NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Weighted prevalence was directly standardized to age distribution (in 10-year age group) of the 2000 Census population. Multivariable-adjusted HR (95% CI) was adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (&lt;high school, high school or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with &lt;20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with &lt;20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: &gt;28 g/day for men/&gt;14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (&lt;5%, 5%–&lt;10%, 10%–&lt;25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45–65% or &gt;65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous), and additionally adjusted for periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing) and number of permanent teeth (0, 1–16, 17–24 or 25–28). Stratification factors were also adjusted within specific strata as appropriate to minimize confounding. For stratification by family poverty income ratio, its continuous variable was adjusted for instead. For stratification by smoking, smoking status (never, past or current) and smoking intensity (pack-years) were adjusted for instead. All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI."}

<meta property="og:image:alt" content="Stratified analyses for untreated caries with risk of all-cause mortality among dentate participants. NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Weighted prevalence was directly standardized to age distribution (in 10-year age group) of the 2000 Census population. Multivariable-adjusted HR (95% CI) was adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (<high school, high school or >high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with <20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with <20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: >28 g/day for men/>14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (<5%, 5%–<10%, 10%–<25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (<45%, 45–65% or >65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous), and additionally adjusted for periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing) and number of permanent teeth (0, 1–16, 17–24 or 25–28). Stratification factors were also adjusted within specific strata as appropriate to minimize confounding. For stratification by family poverty income ratio, its continuous variable was adjusted for instead. For stratification by smoking, smoking status (never, past or current) and smoking intensity (pack-years) were adjusted for instead. All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI."/>

googletag.pubads().addEventListener('slotRenderEnded', function (event) {
                    if (!event.isEmpty) {
                        $('.js-' + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).each(function () {
                            if ($(this).find('iframe').length) {
                                $(this).removeClass('hide');
                            }
                        });
                    }
                });

googletag.pubads().addEventListener('impressionViewable', function (event) {
                    if (!event.isEmpty) {
                        $('.js-' + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).each(function () {
                            var $adblockDiv = $(this).find('.js-adblock');
                            var $adText = $(this).find('.js-adblock-advertisement-text');
                            if ($adblockDiv && $adblockDiv.is(':visible') && $adblockDiv.find('*').length > 1) {
                                $adText.removeClass('hide');
                                App.CenterAdBlock.Init($adblockDiv, $adText);
                            }
                            else {
                                $adText.addClass('hide');
                            }

//Initialize logic for Sticky Footer Ad
                            var $stickyFooterDiv = $(this).parents('.js-sticky-footer-ad');
                            if ($stickyFooterDiv && $stickyFooterDiv.is(':visible') && $stickyFooterDiv.find('*').length > 1) {
                                App.StickyFooterAd.Init();
                            }
                        });
                    }
                });

    $(document).ready(function () {
        $('.article-top-widget').on('click', '.ati-toggle-trigger', function () {
            $(this).find('.icon-general-add, .icon-minus').toggleClass('icon-minus icon-general-add');
            $(this).siblings('.ati-toggle-content').toggleClass('hide');
        });

// In Chrome, an anchor tag with target="_blank" and a "mailto:" href opens a new tab/window as well as the email client
        // I suspect this behavior will be corrected in the future
        // Remove the target="_blank"
        $('ul.wi-affiliationList').find('a[href^="mailto:"]').each(function () {
            $(this).removeAttr('target');
        });
    });
</script>

        </a>
        <ul id="ShareDrop" class="addthis_toolbox addthis_default_style addthis_20x20_style f-dropdown js-dropdown-content" data-dropdown-content>

            <div id="ContentTab" class="content active">
    <div class="widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget">
        <div class="module-widget">
    <div class="widget-items" data-widgetname="ArticleFulltext">

<h2 scrollto-destination="369258257" id="369258257" class="abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title">Abstract</h2>
<section class="abstract"><div class=" sec"><div class="title">Background</div><p class="chapter-para">The link between oral diseases and mortality remains under-explored. We aimed to evaluate the associations between tooth count, untreated caries and risk of all-cause and cause-specific mortality.</p></div><div class=" sec"><div class="title">Methods</div><p class="chapter-para">Data on 24 029 adults from the National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey 1988–94/1999–2010, with mortality linkage to the National Death Index to 31 December 2015, were analysed. Baseline total number of permanent teeth and any untreated caries were assessed by trained dental professionals.</p></div><div class=" sec"><div class="title">Results</div><p class="chapter-para">During up to 27 years of follow-up, 5270 deaths occurred. Fewer permanent teeth were associated with higher all-cause mortality, including heart disease and cancer mortality (all <em>P </em>&lt;0.05 for trend) but not cerebrovascular disease mortality. For every 10 teeth missing, the multivariable-adjusted hazard ratios (HRs) were 1.13 (95% CI: 1.08 to 1.18) for all-cause, 1.16 (95% CI: 1.05, 1.29) for heart disease and 1.19 (95% CI: 1.09, 1.29) for cancer mortality. Untreated caries was associated with increased all-cause (HR: 1.26, 95% CI: 1.15, 1.39) and heart disease mortality (HR: 1.48, 95% CI: 1.17, 1.88) but not cerebrovascular disease/cancer mortality, after adjusting for tooth count, periodontitis and sociodemographic/lifestyle factors. Compared with those without untreated caries and with 25–28 teeth, individuals with untreated caries and 1–16 teeth had a 53% increased risk of all-cause mortality (HR: 1.53, 95% CI: 1.27, 1.85) and 96 % increased risk of heart disease mortality (HR: 1.96, 95% CI: 1.28, 3.01).</p></div><div class=" sec"><div class="title">Conclusions</div><p class="chapter-para">In nationally representative cohorts, fewer permanent teeth and untreated caries were associated with all-cause and heart disease mortality. Fewer teeth were also associated with higher cancer mortality.</p></div></section>                    <div class="article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata"></div>
<div class="kwd-group"><a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="&quot;Oral health&quot;">Oral health</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="caries">caries</a>, <a class="kwd-part kwd-main" href="javascript:;" data-keyword="mortality">mortality</a></div><div id="dyac072-BOX1" class="boxed-text boxed-matter no-caption"><span class="label title-label">Key Messages</span><div class="clearfix"></div><ul class="bullet"><li><p class="chapter-para">In US nationally representative cohorts, every 10 teeth missing was associated with 13% higher risk of all-cause mortality, 16% higher risk of heart disease mortality and 19% higher risk of cancer mortality.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">Untreated caries were associated with 26% increased risk of all-cause mortality and 48% increased risk of heart disease mortality, independent of tooth count and periodontitis.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">These associations were largely consistent within strata of age, sex, race/ethnicity, poverty level, dental insurance, smoking status and consumption of added sugar.</p></li><li><p class="chapter-para">Our findings serve as a significant step in connecting oral health, including common oral disease, with mortality and extends the previous call for integrating oral and general health.</p></li></ul></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258261" id="369258261" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Introduction</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">Oral diseases, primarily dental decay, periodontal disease and subsequent tooth loss, affected more than 3.5 billion people worldwide in 2015.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B1" data-open="dyac072-B1" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>1</sup></a> Despite declines in children, oral diseases remain prevalent in US adults with stark, persistent socioeconomic disparities.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B2" data-open="dyac072-B2" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>2</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B3" data-open="dyac072-B3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>3</sup></a></p><p class="chapter-para">Oral diseases share risk factors such as sugar, alcohol and tobacco consumption with systemic diseases.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B4" data-open="dyac072-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>4</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B5" data-open="dyac072-B5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>5</sup></a> Besides, oral pathogens, their products or pathogen-induced oral inflammatory mediators can trigger host inflammatory responses either locally or at distant body sites.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B6 dyac072-B7 dyac072-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B6 dyac072-B7 dyac072-B8" data-open="dyac072-B6 dyac072-B7 dyac072-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>6–8</sup></a> Periodontal disease, which often involves specific pathogenic bacteria,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B8" data-open="dyac072-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>8</sup></a> has long been associated with cardiovascular disease (CVD)<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B9"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B9" data-open="dyac072-B9" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>9</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B10"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B10" data-open="dyac072-B10" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>10</sup></a> and emerges as a risk factor for diabetes<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B11"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B11" data-open="dyac072-B11" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>11</sup></a> and cancers.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B12" data-open="dyac072-B12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>12</sup></a> Tooth loss, an endpoint that captures the cumulative lifetime history of caries and periodontal disease, has been linked with increased all-cause mortality whereas findings on CVD mortality were mixed.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B13 dyac072-B14 dyac072-B15 dyac072-B16"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B13 dyac072-B14 dyac072-B15 dyac072-B16" data-open="dyac072-B13 dyac072-B14 dyac072-B15 dyac072-B16" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>13–16</sup></a> Further, the association of tooth loss with cancer mortality remains under-explored.</p><p class="chapter-para">Given the modifiable nature of oral diseases, understanding the link between the full spectrum of oral diseases and major chronic diseases/premature mortality is an unmet public health priority.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B5" data-open="dyac072-B5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>5</sup></a> Of particular interest is untreated caries, where overabundant cariogenic bacteria can induce transient bacteremia and subsequent inflammatory injury.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B7" data-open="dyac072-B7" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>7</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B17" data-open="dyac072-B17" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>17</sup></a> Caries is a chronic, progressive condition that tracks across the lifespan.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B18"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B18" data-open="dyac072-B18" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>18</sup></a> Research reporting positive associations between caries and CVD risk has just begun to emerge.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B19" data-open="dyac072-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>19</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B20"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B20" data-open="dyac072-B20" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>20</sup></a> In a large prospective study from Korea, individuals with untreated caries had up to 22% increased risk of major cardiovascular events including cardiac death, myocardial infarction, stroke and heart failure.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B19" data-open="dyac072-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>19</sup></a> Yet, studies in Western populations are limited and have not examined the link between untreated caries and CVD and other cause-specific mortality. The collective influence of tooth loss and untreated caries also remains unknown.</p><p class="chapter-para">To address these knowledge gaps, we examined the associations between tooth count, untreated caries and risk of all-cause and cause-specific mortality among nationally representative samples of US adults, with comprehensive adjustment of potential confounders.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258266" id="369258266" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Methods</h2>
                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258267" id="369258267" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Study population</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">The National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES)<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B21"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B21" data-open="dyac072-B21" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>21</sup></a> surveys a nationally representative sample of the civilian non-institutionalized US population using a complex, stratified, multistage probability sampling design. The NHANES comprises household interviews and physical examinations at a mobile examination centre (MEC), conducted periodically before 1994 and continuously in biennial cycles since 1999. Within each cycle, a subset of participants completed an oral health examination at an MEC and the information collected was used to estimate US prevalence of tooth retention/loss and untreated caries.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B23" data-open="dyac072-B23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>23</sup></a> All NHANES protocols were approved by the National Center for Health Statistics Research Ethics Review Board and all participants provided written informed consent.</p><p class="chapter-para">We restricted the sample to adults aged 30 years or older, who completed tooth count or coronal caries examination in NHANES 1988–94 (NHANES III) and 1999–2010. According to analytical guidelines from NHANES<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B24"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B24" data-open="dyac072-B24" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>24</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B25" data-open="dyac072-B25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>25</sup></a> and prior analyses on the trends of oral health,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a> we separated analyses for NHANES 1988–94 from 1999–2010. We excluded: individuals with self-reported heart disease (excluding angina), cerebrovascular disease or cancer at baseline; without linked mortality data; deaths due to accidents; body mass index (BMI) &lt;18.5 due to potential severe underlying health problems;<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B26" data-open="dyac072-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>26</sup></a>and missing data for covariates except dental insurance and periodontitis. Finally, 24 029 participants (8829 from 1988–94 and 15 200 from 1999–2010) were included (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Figure S1</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258270" id="369258270" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Assessment of tooth count and untreated caries</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Numbers of permanent teeth and untreated caries were evaluated during the dental examination performed by trained dentists (1988–94 and 1999–2004),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B27"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B27" data-open="dyac072-B27" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>27</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B28"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B28" data-open="dyac072-B28" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>28</sup></a> health technologists (2005–08)<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B29"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B29" data-open="dyac072-B29" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>29</sup></a> or dental hygienists (2009–10)<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B30"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B30" data-open="dyac072-B30" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>30</sup></a> using a disposable dental mirror. The number of permanent teeth, including root fragments, was determined by assessing each tooth position of the mouth with a maximum number of 28 (excluding third molars). Since prophylactic extraction of third molars/wisdom teeth is much more common among individuals with high socioeconomic status<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B31"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B31" data-open="dyac072-B31" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>31</sup></a> and wisdom teeth do not represent poor oral health nor have been linked with chronic health outcomes,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B32"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B32" data-open="dyac072-B32" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>32</sup></a> we did not account for wisdom teeth while deriving the total tooth count. We further categorized continuous tooth count into four pre-defined groups [25–28, 17–24, 1–16 and 0 (edentulism)] based on prior publications.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B33"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B33" data-open="dyac072-B33" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>33</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B34"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B34" data-open="dyac072-B34" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>34</sup></a> Untreated caries was defined as the presence of at least one tooth with an untreated coronal carious lesion in 1988–94 and 1999–2008. In 2009–10, untreated caries was determined by examiner’s recommendation for care due to the presence of decayed teeth.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B35"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B35" data-open="dyac072-B35" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>35</sup></a> Reports on quality assurance showed high examiner reliability for the measurement of both tooth count and untreated caries.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30" data-open="dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>27–30</sup></a></p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258272" id="369258272" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Ascertainment of death</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Mortality status was ascertained based on data constructed by the National Center for Health Statistics via probabilistic matching to the National Health Death Index through 31 December 2015.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B36"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B36" data-open="dyac072-B36" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>36</sup></a> Deaths were classified by the 10th revision of International Classification of Diseases (ICD-10). Our primary outcome of interest is all-cause mortality, with heart disease (I00-I09, I11, I13 and I20-I51), cerebrovascular disease (I60-I69), cancer (C00-C97) and other mortality (the rest of the ICD-10 codes) as secondary outcomes.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B37"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B37" data-open="dyac072-B37" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>37</sup></a></p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258274" id="369258274" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Assessment of covariates</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Age, sex, race/ethnicity, family poverty income ratio (PIR; the ratio of family income to poverty guidelines), education, health and dental insurance, leisure-time physical activity and smoking status were self-reported from baseline questionnaires. ‘Ever smoker’ included those who had smoked 100 cigarettes in their life and ‘never smoker’ was defined as those who had not. Pack-years were calculated as the number of cigarettes smoked per day divided by 20 and multiplied by the number of years of smoking. Weight status was determined by BMI based on anthropometric examination. Information on total energy intake, alcohol consumption, calories from added sugar/carbohydrate and Healthy Eating Index<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B38"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B38" data-open="dyac072-B38" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>38</sup></a> (HEI 1994–96 for 1988–94 and HEI-2010 for 1999–2010) were derived from 24-h dietary recalls.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B26" data-open="dyac072-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>26</sup></a> History of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina was defined using self-reported diagnosis, symptoms<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B39"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B39" data-open="dyac072-B39" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>39</sup></a> or treatment. Hypertension was also determined by blood pressure examination, and high cholesterol was determined by laboratory test of total cholesterol.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B40"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B40" data-open="dyac072-B40" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>40</sup></a> Missing indicators for dental insurance were used over 2005–10 due to lack of data. The severity of periodontitis was determined by the Centers of Disease Control and Prevention-American Academy of Periodontology (CDC-AAP) definition based on pocket depth and attachment loss at two interproximal sites (mesio- and mid-facial) for each fully erupted tooth (excluding third molars) in two randomly chosen quadrants (one in the maxillary and one in the mandibular).<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B41"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B41" data-open="dyac072-B41" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>41</sup></a></p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258276" id="369258276" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Statistical analysis</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Sample weights, clustering and stratification were applied to all analyses to account for the complex sampling design of NHANES.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B24"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B24" data-open="dyac072-B24" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>24</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B25" data-open="dyac072-B25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>25</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B37"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B37" data-open="dyac072-B37" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>37</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B42"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B42" data-open="dyac072-B42" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>42</sup></a> Characteristics of the study population were described according to the numbers of permanent teeth and untreated caries. Prevalence of tooth count and untreated caries by selected characteristics was directly standardized to age distribution (in 10-year age groups) of the US 2000 Census population.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B43"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B43" data-open="dyac072-B43" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>43</sup></a> For mortality analyses, person-years accrued from the examination date to the date of death or the end of follow-up (31 December 2015), whichever came first. Cox proportional hazard models were used to calculate hazard ratios (HRs) and 95% confidence intervals (CIs) between the numbers of permanent teeth and untreated caries, respectively, with mortality outcomes of interest. <em>P-</em>values for trend were estimated using tooth count as a continuous variable.</p><p class="chapter-para">The multivariable model for tooth count was adjusted for: age (years), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family PIR (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B25"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B25" data-open="dyac072-B25" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>25</sup></a> education (&lt;high school, high school or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none) and dental insurance (yes, no or missing), personal history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B42"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B42" data-open="dyac072-B42" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>42</sup></a> smoking status (never, past smoker with &lt;20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with &lt;20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none, moderate,or excessive),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B44"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B44" data-open="dyac072-B44" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>44</sup></a> daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (&lt;5%, 5%–&lt;10%, 10%–&lt;25% or ≥25%),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B26" data-open="dyac072-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>26</sup></a> calories from carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45%–65% or &gt;65%),<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B45"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B45" data-open="dyac072-B45" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>45</sup></a> and Healthy Eating Index (quintile) and survey cycle (continuous).</p><p class="chapter-para">As pathogenic bacteria associated with untreated caries are different from periodontal diseases, and most tooth losses are due to periodontal diseases,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B7" data-open="dyac072-B7" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>7</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B8" data-open="dyac072-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>8</sup></a> we hypothesized that untreated caries affects systemic diseases through different biological pathways from the other two oral conditions. Therefore, for analyses of untreated caries among dentate (≥1 tooth) individuals, periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing)<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B41"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B41" data-open="dyac072-B41" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>41</sup></a> and number of permanent teeth were additionally adjusted for to delineate the associations independent of periodontitis and tooth count. Sensitivity analyses that removed hypertension/diabetes from the models were also performed. Proportional hazards assumptions were tested using Schoenfeld residuals, and age (5-year intervals) and sex (female/male) were used as stratum variables. Age- and multivariable-adjusted HRs in each cohort (1988–94 and 1999–2010) were pooled using a fixed-effects model assuming the same true effect, as these two cohorts both represented the US population.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B46"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B46" data-open="dyac072-B46" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>46</sup></a> Sensitivity analysis using a random-effects model was also conducted. E-values, which quantify the minimum effect required for an unmeasured confounder on both exposure and outcome to nullify the observed exposure-outcome associations, were calculated to evaluate the impact of unmeasured confounding.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B47"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B47" data-open="dyac072-B47" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>47</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B48"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B48" data-open="dyac072-B48" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>48</sup></a></p><p class="chapter-para">Joint analyses of untreated caries and tooth count were performed. Stratified analyses for all-cause mortality, according to age, sex, race/ethnicity, family PIR, dental insurance, smoking status and calories from added sugar, were also conducted. Due to possible type I errors induced by multiple comparisons, results of secondary outcome/subgroup analyses were interpreted as exploratory.</p><p class="chapter-para">SAS V9.4 (SAS Institute, NC, USA) and Stata 15.1 (StataCorp, TX, USA) were used for data management and statistical analyses. Two-sided tests with a significance threshold of <em>P</em>&lt;0.05 were applied to all statistical tests.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258282" id="369258282" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Results</h2>
                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258283" id="369258283" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Characteristics of the participants</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">A total of 24 029 participants were included in the analyses. Older participants tended to have lower numbers of permanent teeth but similar untreated caries compared with younger adults (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-T1"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-T1" data-open="dyac072-T1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Table 1;</a> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S1</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online). Participants with fewer teeth were more likely to have lower family PIR, lower levels of education and no private health insurance or dental insurance. They were also more likely to have diabetes, hypertension or angina, to be obese, physically inactive, past or current heavy smokers or non-drinkers, and to consume a low-quality diet. These patterns were similar for participants with untreated caries. In addition, the prevalence of untreated caries was substantially higher among men, non-Hispanic Blacks and Hispanics, individuals with periodontitis and those with higher added sugar intake. Compared with adults in 1988–94, US adults retained more teeth, with 7.1% edentulous and 9.3% with 1–16 permanent teeth, in 1999–2010 (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S2</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online). The prevalence of untreated caries has declined from 25.3% to 21.0% during the same period, primarily driven by improvement in the older population (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S3</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online).</p>                    <a id="369258285" scrollto-destination="369258285"></a>

content-id="tblfn1" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><p class="chapter-para">NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; SE, standard error.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn2"></span><div content-id="tblfn2" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn2" class="end-note-link">a</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Sample weights, clustering and stratification were applied to all analyses to account for the complex sampling design of NHANES.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn3"></span><div content-id="tblfn3" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn3" class="end-note-link">b</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Analyses for untreated caries were conducted among the dentate population.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn4"></span><div content-id="tblfn4" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn4" class="end-note-link">c</span></span><p class="chapter-para">‘Other’ included race/ethnicity other than non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black or Hispanic, including multiracial.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn5"></span><div content-id="tblfn5" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn5" class="end-note-link">d</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Percentage was calculated among participants without missing values. Missing indicator for dental insurance was used between 2005–10 due to lack of data.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn6"></span><div content-id="tblfn6" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn6" class="end-note-link">e</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Diabetes was defined as self-report of diagnosis or currently taking medication for treatment. Hypertension was defined as systolic blood pressure ≥140 mmHg or diastolic blood pressure ≥90 mmHg among at least three consecutive determinations or self-report of diagnosis. High cholesterol was defined as blood total cholesterol ≥6.2 mmol/L or self-report of diagnosis. Angina was defined as self-report of symptoms (NHANES 1988–94) or diagnosis (NHANES 1999–2010).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn7"></span><div content-id="tblfn7" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn7" class="end-note-link">f</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Percentage was calculated among participants without missing values. ‘NA’ represents no observation in this category. As periodontal assessment was not available for 2005–08 and changed substantially in 2009–10, missing indicator for periodontitis was used for 2005–10.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn8"></span><div content-id="tblfn8" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn8" class="end-note-link">g</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Weight status was defined by body mass index, calculated as weight in kilograms divided by height in metres squared. Standard cutoffs (normal weight, 18.5–24.9; overweight, 25–29.9; obese, ≥30) were used.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn9"></span><div content-id="tblfn9" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn9" class="end-note-link">h</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Leisure-time physical activity was defined by engaging any moderate or vigorous recreational physical activity over the past 30 days (1988–94 and 1999–2006) or in a typical week (2006–10) (yes/no).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn10"></span><div content-id="tblfn10" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn10" class="end-note-link">i</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Ever smoker was defined as individuals who smoked ≥100 cigarettes in life. Pack-years among ever smokers was calculated as the number of cigarettes smoked per day divided by 20 and multiply the number of years of smoking.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn11"></span><div content-id="tblfn11" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn11" class="end-note-link">j</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Alcohol consumption was defined by total intake of alcohol during the 24-hour period prior to the NHANES dietary interview (none: 0 g/d, moderate: 0.1-≤28 g/d for men/0.1-≤14 g/d for women, or excessive: &gt;28 g/d for men/&gt;14 g/d for women).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn12"></span><div content-id="tblfn12" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn12" class="end-note-link">k</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Healthy Eating Index (HEI) 1994-1996 was used for NHANES 1988-1994; HEI-2010 was used for NHANES 1999-2010.</p></span></div></div></div></div><div class="table-full-width-wrap"><div class="table-wrap table-wide standard-table"><div class="table-wrap-title" id="dyac072-T1" data-id="dyac072-T1"><span class="label title-label" id="label-84544">Table 1</span><div class="

(6.8)</td><td>876 (15.9)</td><td>1049 (13.0)</td><td>1072 (15.7)</td><td>1021 (6.1)</td><td>1038 (13.2)</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="table-wrap-foot"><span id="fn-tblfn1"></span><div content-id="tblfn1" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><p class="chapter-para">NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; SE, standard error.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn2"></span><div content-id="tblfn2" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn2" class="end-note-link">a</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Sample weights, clustering and stratification were applied to all analyses to account for the complex sampling design of NHANES.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn3"></span><div content-id="tblfn3" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn3" class="end-note-link">b</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Analyses for untreated caries were conducted among the dentate population.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn4"></span><div content-id="tblfn4" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn4" class="end-note-link">c</span></span><p class="chapter-para">‘Other’ included race/ethnicity other than non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black or Hispanic, including multiracial.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn5"></span><div content-id="tblfn5" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn5" class="end-note-link">d</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Percentage was calculated among participants without missing values. Missing indicator for dental insurance was used between 2005–10 due to lack of data.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn6"></span><div content-id="tblfn6" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn6" class="end-note-link">e</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Diabetes was defined as self-report of diagnosis or currently taking medication for treatment. Hypertension was defined as systolic blood pressure ≥140 mmHg or diastolic blood pressure ≥90 mmHg among at least three consecutive determinations or self-report of diagnosis. High cholesterol was defined as blood total cholesterol ≥6.2 mmol/L or self-report of diagnosis. Angina was defined as self-report of symptoms (NHANES 1988–94) or diagnosis (NHANES 1999–2010).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn7"></span><div content-id="tblfn7" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn7" class="end-note-link">f</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Percentage was calculated among participants without missing values. ‘NA’ represents no observation in this category. As periodontal assessment was not available for 2005–08 and changed substantially in 2009–10, missing indicator for periodontitis was used for 2005–10.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn8"></span><div content-id="tblfn8" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn8" class="end-note-link">g</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Weight status was defined by body mass index, calculated as weight in kilograms divided by height in metres squared. Standard cutoffs (normal weight, 18.5–24.9; overweight, 25–29.9; obese, ≥30) were used.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn9"></span><div content-id="tblfn9" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn9" class="end-note-link">h</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Leisure-time physical activity was defined by engaging any moderate or vigorous recreational physical activity over the past 30 days (1988–94 and 1999–2006) or in a typical week (2006–10) (yes/no).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn10"></span><div content-id="tblfn10" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn10" class="end-note-link">i</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Ever smoker was defined as individuals who smoked ≥100 cigarettes in life. Pack-years among ever smokers was calculated as the number of cigarettes smoked per day divided by 20 and multiply the number of years of smoking.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn11"></span><div content-id="tblfn11" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn11" class="end-note-link">j</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Alcohol consumption was defined by total intake of alcohol during the 24-hour period prior to the NHANES dietary interview (none: 0 g/d, moderate: 0.1-≤28 g/d for men/0.1-≤14 g/d for women, or excessive: &gt;28 g/d for men/&gt;14 g/d for women).</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn12"></span><div content-id="tblfn12" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn12" class="end-note-link">k</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Healthy Eating Index (HEI) 1994-1996 was used for NHANES 1988-1994; HEI-2010 was used for NHANES 1999-2010.</p></span></div></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258286" id="369258286" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Tooth count and mortality</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">During up to 27 years of follow-up (173 402 person-years from NHANES 1988–94 and 151 216 from NHANES 1999–2010), 5270 deaths occurred, including 1080 from heart disease, 315 from cerebrovascular disease, 1207 from cancer and 2634 from other causes. We first examined the association between tooth count and mortality. Inverse dose-response associations between the number of permanent teeth and all-cause mortality were observed, driven by deaths from heart disease, cancer and other causes but not from cerebrovascular disease (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-T2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-T2" data-open="dyac072-T2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Table 2;</a> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S4</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online). Compared with individuals with 25–28 teeth, the pooled multivariable-adjusted HRs for all-cause mortality were 1.19 (95% CI: 1.07 to 1.33) for 17 to 24 teeth, 1.30 (95% CI: 1.15 to 1.46) for 1 to 16 teeth, and 1.43 (95% CI: 1.26 to 1.63) for edentulism (<em>P </em>&lt;0.001 for trend). For every 10 teeth missing, the HRs were 1.13 (95% CI: 1.08 to 1.18) for all-cause mortality, 1.16 (95% CI: 1.05 to 1.29) for heart disease mortality, 1.19 (95% CI: 1.09 to 1.29) for cancer mortality and 1.10 (95% CI: 1.04 to 1.17) for other mortality.</p>                    <a id="369258288" scrollto-destination="369258288"></a>

class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn16" class="end-note-link">c</span></span><p class="chapter-para"><em>P-</em>value for trend was calculated using number of permanent teeth as a continuous variable.</p></span></div></div></div></div><p class="chapter-para">The inverse associations between the numbers of permanent teeth and all-cause mortality were similar regardless of baseline age (&lt;70 vs ≥70), sex (male vs female), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White vs non-Hispanic Black/Hispanic), family PIR (≤1.30 vs &gt;1.30), dental insurance (no vs yes), smoking status (never smoker vs ever smoker) and calories from added sugar (&lt;10% vs ≥10%) (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258290" id="369258290" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Untreated caries and mortality</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Among dentate adults, we also examined the association between untreated caries and mortality. Untreated caries was associated with an increased risk of all-cause mortality after adjusting for the same set of covariates as for tooth count (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-T3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-T3" data-open="dyac072-T3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Table 3;</a> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S6</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online). Of note, these estimates were similar after additionally adjusting for periodontitis and tooth count. Compared with participants without untreated caries, individuals with untreated caries had a 26% increased risk of all-cause mortality (HR: 1.26, 95% CI: 1.15 to 1.39), driven by a higher risk of death from heart disease (HR: 1.48, 95% CI: 1.17 to 1.88) and other causes (HR: 1.22, 95% CI: 1.07 to 1.39) but not from cerebrovascular disease (HR: 1.18, 95% CI: 0.85 to 1.65) and cancer (HR: 1.18, 95% CI: 0.98 to 1.42). The positive associations between untreated caries and all-cause mortality were similarly observed within subgroups of age, sex, race/ethnicity, family PIR, dental insurance, smoking status and calories from added sugar (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-F1"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="dyac072-F1" class="link xref-fig">Figure 1;</a> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S7</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online).</p>                    <a id="369258292" scrollto-destination="369258292"></a>
<div data-id="dyac072-f1" data-content-id="dyac072-f1" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section" swap-content-for-modal="true"><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343im_/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/m_dyac072f1.jpeg?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=YSJcSdNTnAo3iQWO2rx-QoAVXeuIBHoksBlOW4Ipp-~jOj32~cwmPHRXw-ayz2UsWbGUSSsmM-ql2HZNeL~QUPDOt-1IFMOZEtAQUE6~inZBk2WQfarbVVDFXA2ScfBb7KH9yKZoiKMCpqp4dCERajOxedRSPSLvSVfMMusZzu1ha41j8jns~3fTilo~UfUeWI0dUhLUMu1emRrlu65oModIS9969X7D-boYmQOPEKJiAqPKaIr6c~cRafiFalXJU9CMskHyp7BbzRJG3uGEvjGFdB8VP1P4FnoraTglrrK~lS~il-AuIWyRjKey2IayFpuu4deRLR5vqVRrM8IRpg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Stratified analyses for untreated caries with risk of all-cause mortality among dentate participants. NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Weighted prevalence was directly standardized to age distribution (in 10-year age group) of the 2000 Census population. Multivariable-adjusted HR (95% CI) was adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (<high school, high school or >high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with <20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with <20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: >28 g/day for men/>14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (<5%, 5%–<10%, 10%–<25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (<45%, 45–65% or >65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous), and additionally adjusted for periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing) and number of permanent teeth (0, 1–16, 17–24 or 25–28). Stratification factors were also adjusted within specific strata as appropriate to minimize confounding. For stratification by family poverty income ratio, its continuous variable was adjusted for instead. For stratification by smoking, smoking status (never, past or current) and smoking intensity (pack-years) were adjusted for instead. All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI." data-path-from-xml="dyac072f1.tif"/><div class="graphic-bottom"><div class="label fig-label" id="label-369258292">Figure 1</div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Stratified analyses for untreated caries with risk of all-cause mortality among dentate participants. NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Weighted prevalence was directly standardized to age distribution (in 10-year age group) of the 2000 Census population. Multivariable-adjusted HR (95% CI) was adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (&lt;high school, high school or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with &lt;20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with &lt;20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: &gt;28 g/day for men/&gt;14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (&lt;5%, 5%–&lt;10%, 10%–&lt;25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45–65% or &gt;65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous), and additionally adjusted for periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing) and number of permanent teeth (0, 1–16, 17–24 or 25–28). Stratification factors were also adjusted within specific strata as appropriate to minimize confounding. For stratification by family poverty income ratio, its continuous variable was adjusted for instead. For stratification by smoking, smoking status (never, past or current) and smoking intensity (pack-years) were adjusted for instead. All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI.</p></div><div class="ajax-articleAbstract-exclude-regex fig-orig original-slide figure-button-wrap"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large at-figureViewLarge openInAnotherWindow" role="button" aria-describedby="label-369258292" href="/web/20250819143343/https://academic.oup.com/view-large/figure/369258292/dyac072f1.tif" data-path-from-xml="dyac072f1.tif" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a class="download-slide" role="button" aria-describedby="label-369258292" data-section="369258292" href="/web/20250819143343/https://academic.oup.com/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072f1.jpeg?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=QnLNFAjShmFMgdiP0f9PZJ2Gfip4sygqSYwiEbHoja36RTJibEweDGTIOCoQokR0JwTZLGiU6BVbsVv-m5ePTxSFnxTV-HJSl6fr-bR1VGE70gEZ1dPoH1AutAugLgg~SwmUlX9gMMKd~SBElj6W8E4MBfQMyU-FQhyg-e6WE4vsHNON1x9~vd6Evv8AnSdwARCtq8LCzXndZ3q5vSVdBS65JkZfSE9jAWGr38QlcyuIBPFBRRwjWSRv1RESpA02WjQdW-GTKnhKccwc0ER19PONZn35gfDK~wHkPChRHlEKNqhISc4QPcCkG-HSwDoJ7uu-lNPylNKwvDnQ~Ftf9A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&amp;sec=369258292&amp;ar=6564484&amp;xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&amp;imagename=&amp;siteId=5299" data-path-from-xml="dyac072f1.tif">Download slide</a></div></div></div></div>                    <a id="369258293" scrollto-destination="369258293"></a>

(&lt;high school, high school, or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non–private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45%–65%,or &gt;65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous).
