was adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non–Hispanic White, non–Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (&lt;high school, high school, or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non–private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure–time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with &lt;20 pack–years, past smoker with ≥20 pack–years, current smoker with &lt;20 pack–years or current smoker with ≥20 pack–years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/d, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/d for men/0.1–≤14 g/d for women, excessive: &gt;28 g/d for men/&gt;14 g/d for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (&lt;5%, 5%–&lt;10%, 10%–&lt;25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45%–65%,or &gt;65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile), and survey cycle (continuous). Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model.</p></span></div><span id="fn-tblfn20"></span><div content-id="tblfn20" class="footnote"><span class="fn"><span class="label fn-label"><span rel="nofollow" data-fn-id="tblfn20" class="end-note-link">c</span></span><p class="chapter-para">Additionally adjusted for periodontitis (none, mild, moderate, severe or missing) and number of permanent teeth (0, 1–16, 17–24 or 25–28).</p></span></div></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258294" id="369258294" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Joint analyses of tooth count and untreated carries with mortality</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">In joint analyses of untreated caries and tooth count, individuals with untreated caries had an elevated risk of all-cause, heart disease and other mortality compared with those without untreated caries within each tooth count stratum (<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-F2"></span><a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="dyac072-F2" class="link xref-fig">Figure 2;</a> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S8</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online). Compared with participants without untreated caries and with 25–28 teeth, individuals with untreated caries and 1–16 teeth had a 53% increased risk of all-cause mortality (HR: 1.53, 95% CI: 1.27 to 1.85), 96% increased risk of heart disease mortality (HR: 1.96, 95% CI: 1.28 to 3.01) and 48% increased risk of other mortality (HR: 1.48, 95% CI: 1.16 to 1.90).</p>                    <a id="369258296" scrollto-destination="369258296"></a>
<div data-id="dyac072-f2" data-content-id="dyac072-f2" class="fig fig-section js-fig-section" swap-content-for-modal="true"><div class="graphic-wrap"><img class="content-image" src="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343im_/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/m_dyac072f2.jpeg?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=YwPxSmqZdUCyG4Khv2oxbl9sfAJIe2hI~wuPzyhA-e3Tyyhrgpx~RvKlGEX~nRoDskQ-DbG7JQHFrESqJoK8nSdS5v2T0YVjpaQTtlNx4RQ~beh6sAcgfY7zUmNhOKHYSpZrj7uwj65N3rr1TqVXoMzJlsrv3Ryc67uSBxbpY6dCgvUGNvH9yYjesYEmjvRe45yF0cbO2gIC5XjxdnAGc9H7ZaO~QpkrYJcda5jEVlxN9AOK21Mgl4lT72YfMY2ynlqixqxrF4K0Gy4~1VmD5ABt4S3K7qiWUzKpZjL52ZpfKGUT6HuzY5~dlJFCX1bPCJSz-vDwJ3BLvAWA3tPXIw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA" alt="Joint associations for untreated caries and number of permanent teeth with risk of all-cause mortality. NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Multivariable hazard ratios for joint associations between untreated caries, number of permanent teeth and risk of all-cause mortality were conducted among dentate participants and adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (<high school, high school or >high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with <20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with <20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: >28 g/day for men/>14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (<5%, 5–<10%, 10–<25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (<45%, 45–65% or >65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile) and survey cycle (continuous). All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI." data-path-from-xml="dyac072f2.tif"/><div class="graphic-bottom"><div class="label fig-label" id="label-369258296">Figure 2</div><div class="caption fig-caption"><p class="chapter-para">Joint associations for untreated caries and number of permanent teeth with risk of all-cause mortality. NHANES, National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey; HR, hazard ratio; CI, confidence interval. Multivariable hazard ratios for joint associations between untreated caries, number of permanent teeth and risk of all-cause mortality were conducted among dentate participants and adjusted for age (year), sex (female/male), race/ethnicity (non-Hispanic White, non-Hispanic Black, Hispanic or others), family poverty income ratio (0–1.30, 1.31–3.50 or ≥3.51), education (&lt;high school, high school or &gt;high school), health insurance (private, non-private or none), dental insurance (yes, no or missing), history of diabetes, hypertension, high cholesterol and angina (for each disease, yes/no), weight status (normal, BMI: 18.5–24.9; overweight, BMI: 25–29.9; obese, BMI ≥30), leisure-time physical activity (yes/no), smoking status (never, past smoker with &lt;20 pack-years, past smoker with ≥20 pack-years, current smoker with &lt;20 pack-years or current smoker with ≥20 pack-years), alcohol consumption (none: 0 g/day, moderate: 0.1–≤28 g/day for men/0.1–≤14 g/day for women, excessive: &gt;28 g/day for men/&gt;14 g/day for women), daily energy intake (kcal), calories from added sugar (&lt;5%, 5–&lt;10%, 10–&lt;25% or ≥25%), calories from carbohydrate (&lt;45%, 45–65% or &gt;65%), Healthy Eating Index (quintile) and survey cycle (continuous). All analyses were stratified by age (5-year group) and sex. Results from NHANES 1988–94 and NHANES 1999–2010 with follow-up to 2015 were combined using a fixed-effects model. Error bars show 95% CI.</p></div><div class="ajax-articleAbstract-exclude-regex fig-orig original-slide figure-button-wrap"><a class="fig-view-orig js-view-large at-figureViewLarge openInAnotherWindow" role="button" aria-describedby="label-369258296" href="/web/20250819143343/https://academic.oup.com/view-large/figure/369258296/dyac072f2.tif" data-path-from-xml="dyac072f2.tif" target="_blank">Open in new tab</a><a class="download-slide" role="button" aria-describedby="label-369258296" data-section="369258296" href="/web/20250819143343/https://academic.oup.com/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072f2.jpeg?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=prvaH4qvwWuPbNDDzPTcq6G5i6h9osmxeBKr0IWXxrzbhttQWw3uEjvkA99s~Gj7TnAyxwyBgNaJ33ISDuka1Uyidhs4GMe4rWC9dReKmk6WwFiBLXtcuoTtVL77ff3898o9FIMHZcAPvHUgW0MiTeW5TXtMfNZKLx9hk01yaeuJStmMjDgK8z1Jx6C9YaJBGEyXpA0pZdow1kEN~CSojtXIorxpS39SwOUM0UZ3fQCcL-8Saobsbh3ssl7B1Y~uL9WctAn2wVIMqcw3mQivYXvEx~VsURfbqTGNygCzf5yG1RLP0QIOhMKFixy60ppbipa~rYB0tAcHlyDbtWpIGw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&amp;sec=369258296&amp;ar=6564484&amp;xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&amp;imagename=&amp;siteId=5299" data-path-from-xml="dyac072f2.tif">Download slide</a></div></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination="369258297" id="369258297" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Sensitivity analyses</h3>
<p class="chapter-para">Results from the random-effects model were largely consistent with estimates from the fixed-effect model (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S4</a></span> and <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S6</a></span>). An unmeasured confounder needs to be associated with both tooth count and all-cause mortality with an HR of at least 1.30 to nullify this association. The least HR for an unmeasured confounder to nullify the association between untreated caries and all-cause mortality is 1.44. Since we have adjusted for a wide range of confounders in our analyses, unmeasured confounders were less likely to have associations with both the exposure and the mortality endpoints that exceed the corresponding E-values (<span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary Table S9</a></span>, available as <span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> at <em>IJE</em> online).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258299" id="369258299" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Discussion</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">In this large, prospective study representing US adults, having fewer permanent teeth was associated with increased risk of all-cause mortality, after considering a wide range of sociodemographic and lifestyle factors. This positive association was driven by deaths from heart disease, cancer and other causes. Untreated caries was also associated with increased risks of all-cause, heart disease and other mortality, independent of tooth count and periodontitis. These associations were largely consistent within strata of age, sex, race/ethnicity, poverty level, dental insurance, smoking status and consumption of added sugar.</p><p class="chapter-para">A meta-analysis suggests that losses of 10, 20 and 32 teeth were associated with 15%, 33% and 57% increased risk of all-cause mortality, respectively.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B16"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B16" data-open="dyac072-B16" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>16</sup></a> However, evidence was mixed for CVD mortality, which may be in part attributable to incomplete adjustment for confounders such as socioeconomic factors<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B49"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B49" data-open="dyac072-B49" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>49</sup></a> and smoking.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B50"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B50" data-open="dyac072-B50" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>50</sup></a> Our robust findings after adjusting for a list of confounding factors and similar findings within each stratum collectively lend strong support to the observed association between lower tooth count and increased risks of both total and heart disease mortality, whereas the lack of association with cerebrovascular disease mortality requires further validations. In line with our findings on cancer mortality, a recent analysis in NHANES 1988–94 found a 50% higher mortality from cancer when comparing edentulous adults with a dentate population without periodontal disease.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B34"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B34" data-open="dyac072-B34" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>34</sup></a> Our study expands these evaluations by additionally integrating NHANES 1999–2010 to elucidate the dose-response relationship between fewer teeth and increased risk of cancer mortality, in the context of mortality from other causes.</p><p class="chapter-para">The association between untreated caries and mortality was not well studied. In an Iranian cohort, DMFT index, a score capturing decayed (D), missing (M) and filled (F) teeth (T), was associated with an increased risk of all-cause and cancer mortality, but not CVD mortality.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B14"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B14" data-open="dyac072-B14" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>14</sup></a> However, DMFT captures both tooth loss and caries, without differentiating untreated caries. Indeed, untreated caries has been used by the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention as a population-level indicator for oral health burden.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B3" data-open="dyac072-B3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>3</sup></a> In line with our observed positive association between untreated caries and heart disease mortality, a recent large prospective study in Korea found that untreated caries was associated with a 7–22% increased risk of major cardiovascular events including cardiac death, myocardial infarction, stroke and heart failure.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B19" data-open="dyac072-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>19</sup></a> Instead of using a composite endpoint, our findings extend the scope of Park <em>et al.</em> by looking through various endpoints and observing a positive association between untreated caries and all-cause mortality, driven by deaths from heart disease and other causes but not cerebrovascular disease and cancers, independent of a range of potential confounders. More importantly, the positive association remained similar after adjusting for both tooth count and periodontitis and was similarly observed within each stratum of tooth count. Collectively, these findings provide preliminary but compelling evidence that untreated caries may operate through a pathway independent of periodontitis/tooth loss to increase risk of premature death.</p><p class="chapter-para">Our findings linking untreated caries and all-cause and heart disease mortality are biologically plausible. <em>Streptococcus mutans</em>, a major cariogenic species in dental caries,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B18"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B18" data-open="dyac072-B18" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>18</sup></a> was also found to be the dominant species in atherosclerotic plaque.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B51"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B51" data-open="dyac072-B51" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>51</sup></a> Interestingly in the same patient, its abundance in atherosclerotic plaques correlated with that in oral cavities.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B17"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B17" data-open="dyac072-B17" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>17</sup></a> It has been postulated that <em>Streptococcus mutans</em> and its products may spread to the bloodstream and promote plaque formation through directly invading vascular endothelium or inducing systemic immune response, thereby increasing the risk of heart disease.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B7"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B7" data-open="dyac072-B7" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>7</sup></a> Throughout the progression of periodontitis, the oral microbiota was modified and many highly pathogenic bacteria, including <em>Porphyromonas gingivalis</em>, <em>Treponema denticola</em> and <em>Tannerella forsythia</em>, were over-represented.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B8" data-open="dyac072-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>8</sup></a> In addition to CVD,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B9"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B9" data-open="dyac072-B9" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>9</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B10"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B10" data-open="dyac072-B10" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>10</sup></a> these bacterial species could also potentially facilitate the development of oral, oesophageal, lung, pancreas, colon/rectum, breast, prostate and haematological cancers,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B12" data-open="dyac072-B12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>12</sup></a> with provoking chronic systemic inflammation as the leading putative mechanism.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B8"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B8" data-open="dyac072-B8" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>8</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B12"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B12" data-open="dyac072-B12" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>12</sup></a> Tooth loss as an endpoint of periodontitis might further promote CVD and cancer by harbouring a more disturbed oral microbiota. It is also likely that oral diseases are predictors of poor health behaviours and perceptions. Shared risk factors between oral diseases and mortality, such as socioeconomic factors,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B49"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B49" data-open="dyac072-B49" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>49</sup></a> smoking<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B50"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B50" data-open="dyac072-B50" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>50</sup></a> and diet,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B26"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B26" data-open="dyac072-B26" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>26</sup></a> might be the underlying causal factors. The strength of associations between tooth loss, untreated caries and mortality were attenuated after adjusting for potential confounders, particularly family PIR, dental insurance, smoking and added sugar consumption. However, stratified analyses within strata of these confounders suggest that our observed associations cannot be fully explained by measured confounding, although unmeasured confounding could not be ruled out. Information on potential unmeasured confounders, like oral hygiene and dental visits, need to be included in future studies.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B19"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B19" data-open="dyac072-B19" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>19</sup></a></p><p class="chapter-para">Our findings reflect a significant step in connecting oral health and mortality. Indeed, oral health has been largely neglected in both high-income and low/middle-income countries.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B4" data-open="dyac072-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>4</sup></a> According to our analyses and prior studies, in the USA untreated caries is common, with minimal improvement among adults especially younger adults aged 20–64 over the past two decades.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B3" data-open="dyac072-B3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>3</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a> Moreover, about half of adults are at higher risk of tooth loss due to periodontal disease.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B2" data-open="dyac072-B2" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>2</sup></a> Low-income, uninsured and racial/ethnic minority populations suffer from higher burden of oral diseases throughout the life course,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B2"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B2" data-open="dyac072-B2" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>2</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B3"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B3" data-open="dyac072-B3" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>3</sup></a> due to limited access to dental care and disproportionate exposures to an array of individual, social and commercial determinants.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B4" data-open="dyac072-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>4</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B52"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B52" data-open="dyac072-B52" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>52</sup></a> However, oral health disparities have not yet been recognized as contributing to the disproportional burden of mortality among these groups. Joint efforts from public and private sectors are needed immediately to expand oral health-related educational/outreach programmes, improve equal access to dental care<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B5"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B5" data-open="dyac072-B5" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>5</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B52"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B52" data-open="dyac072-B52" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>52</sup></a> and integrate dental care into general health practices.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B4"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B4" data-open="dyac072-B4" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>4</sup></a></p><p class="chapter-para">Our study has a few notable strengths. First, our study maximally utilized the oral health examination data as well as mortality linkage from the NHANES since 1988, to assess the association between oral health and mortality prospectively. Second, the oral health information was assessed using standardized protocols within each survey cycle with high examiner reliability,<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30" data-open="dyac072-B27 dyac072-B28 dyac072-B29 dyac072-B30" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>27–30</sup></a> which minimized the measurement errors. Third, our comprehensive adjustment for potential confounders and subgroup-specific analyses minimized the influence of confounding. Finally, the use of nationally representative samples ensured the generalizability of our findings to the US population.</p><p class="chapter-para">Our study also has limitations. First, in the NHANES, untreated caries was assessed with a disposable mirror by various dental professionals and slightly differently in 2009–10. However, the protocols have been standardized and these data were used to estimate the prevalence and temporal trends of untreated caries in the USA.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B23"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B23" data-open="dyac072-B23" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>23</sup></a> Further, such non-differential misclassification, if any, would likely bias the association towards the null. Second, partial-mouth periodontal examination might underestimate the prevalence of periodontitis.<span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B22"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B22" data-open="dyac072-B22" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>22</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B27"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B27" data-open="dyac072-B27" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>27</sup></a><sup>,</sup><span class="xrefLink" id="jumplink-dyac072-B28"></span><a href="javascript:;" reveal-id="dyac072-B28" data-open="dyac072-B28" class="link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr"><sup>28</sup></a> Although including tooth count in the models for untreated caries could allow us to adjust for most effects of periodontitis, data from full-mouth examination at six sites are warranted in future study. Third, the NHANES has not collected updated information on tooth loss, untreated caries and periodontal diseases during follow-up period. With only baseline measurement, we might have misclassified people who developed oral diseases later on as unexposed. Such non-differential misclassification may bias the associations towards the null. Studies with updated oral health exposures and other covariates are warranted. Also, the lack of information on reasons for teeth missing (including wisdom teeth) and the severity of untreated caries limited our ability to further differentiate the effects of exposures. Additionally, we have limited power to look into details of mortality from specific cancer types and other causes which include respiratory diseases, Alzheimer’s disease, diabetes and so forth. Finally, the role of time-varying and unmeasured confounding could not be ruled out.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258307" id="369258307" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Conclusions</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">In nationally representative cohorts of US adults, fewer permanent teeth were associated with all-cause mortality including heart disease and cancer mortality, with dose-response relationships. Untreated dental caries, a previously under-appreciated benign oral disease, was linked with increased risks of all-cause, heart disease and other mortality. The general public should be informed and encouraged to protect their oral health proactively. Health professionals should be aware of the link between oral and general health and should integrate dental care into general health practices. Larger prospective studies with detailed and time-dependent assessments of exposures and covariates are warranted for validating our findings.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258309" id="369258309" class="section-title js-splitscreen-section-title">Ethics approval</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">All NHANES protocols were approved by the National Center for Health Statistics Research Ethics Review Board, and all participants provided documented consent: detailed information at [<a class="link link-uri openInAnotherWindow" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/nhanes/irba98.htm]" target="_blank">https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/nhanes/irba98.htm]</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258311" id="369258311" class="dataavailabilitystatement-title js-splitscreen-dataavailabilitystatement-title">Data Availability</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">Data, documentation and codebook of the National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey are available for download at [<a class="link link-uri openInAnotherWindow" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/nhanes/index.htm]" target="_blank">https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/nhanes/index.htm]</a>. Statistical code for this study is available upon request from YC [<a href="/web/20250819143343/https://academic.oup.com/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection#9ce5f5f2b2fffdf3dcebe9efe8f0b2f9f8e9" target="_blank"><span class="__cf_email__" data-cfemail="fb829295d5989a94bb8c8e888f97d59e9f8e">[email&#160;protected]</span></a>].</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258313" id="369258313" class="backsection-title js-splitscreen-backsection-title">Supplementary data</h2>
<p class="chapter-para"><span class="link link-data-supplement" data-supplement-target="sup1"></span><span class="content-section supplementary-material"><a path-from-xml="sup1" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20250819143343/https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/ije/51/4/10.1093_ije_dyac072/2/dyac072_supplementary_data.pdf?Expires=1758638025&amp;Signature=mmf0NL07Q8hwd42VVMoeL0akUydPJ8M-YY-Xa6B70mfYN-hJ3yNTMZ7uAZdTGp1cil5QB12Cx3QTbSuOCQPeUuAj0qYLk7i2s7FmysQwDQ1HRJ6k5-UFLwAM0y6HMoySUYyTt0PL5ygQVq9SvbrjBKTcxkGDOBENeqRMlLUyoCDK-az-JyduDE1eMc6~eSZe7Mp15G9gZvcq7j0dH-~c3EFcviVngPYvM~MDft-pb38ZoARJdusO~arhY8LBKtKUrJWLzWCQ7LNrxbxS3MSe3WycdYMuqEN3Bf-KvzUYI3HbEMN~ZgoYVT4uRBOwrj7GjK~q9QW9dMeRufUXqHy94w__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA">Supplementary data</a></span> are available at <em>IJE</em> online.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258315" id="369258315" class="backsection-title js-splitscreen-backsection-title">Author contributions</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">J.L. and Y.C. had full access to all the data in the study and take responsibility for the integrity of the data and the accuracy of the data analysis. J.L. and Y.C. were involved in study concept and design. All authors were involved in acquisition, analysis or interpretation of data. J.L., C.C.A. and Y.C. drafted the manuscript. All authors drafted critical revisions of the manuscript for important intellectual content. J.L., X.Z. and Y.C. performed the statistical analysis. Y.C. supervised the study and provided administrative, technical and material support. C.C.A. and Y.C. contributed equally.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258317" id="369258317" class="backsection-title js-splitscreen-backsection-title">Funding</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">This work was supported by the U.S. National Institutes of Health (NIH) grant P30 CA091842. Y.C. is supported by the Siteman Investment Program, Washington University. E.V. and C.C.A. were supported by the Intramural Research Program of the National Cancer Institute. A.T.C. is a Stuart and Suzanne Steele Massachusetts General Hospital (MGH) Research Scholar. The funders had no role in the design and conduct of the study; collection, management, analysis and interpretation of the data; preparation, review and approval of the manuscript.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258319" id="369258319" class="backsection-title js-splitscreen-backsection-title">Conflict of interest</h2>
<p class="chapter-para">None declared.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination="369258321" id="369258321" class="backreferences-title js-splitscreen-backreferences-title" data-legacy-id="ref1">References</h2>
